<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Study of general and fundamental questions</div>

<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="text-align:center; background:#ddddff;"><div class="sidebar-list-title-c"><span style="font-size:110%"><a href="/wiki/Category:Philosophy-related_lists" title="Category:Philosophy-related lists">Lists</a></span></div></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content">

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below" style="border-top:1px solid #aaa;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;">
<img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="326" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" width="10"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template talk:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Philosophy_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Philosophy</b> (from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">φιλοσοφία</span></span>, <span title="Ancient Greek-language text"><i lang="grc">philosophia</i></span>, 'love of wisdom')<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Online_Etymology_Dictionary_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Online_Etymology_Dictionary-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster's_New_World_Dictionary_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webster's_New_World_Dictionary-2">[2]</a></sup> is the systematized study of general and fundamental questions, such as those about <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">knowledge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">values</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">mind</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_language" title="Philosophy of language">language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Such questions are often posed as problems<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> to be studied or resolved. Some sources claim the term was coined by <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a> (c. 570 – c. 495 BCE);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> others dispute this story,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> arguing that Pythagoreans merely claimed use of a preexisting term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_methodology" title="Philosophical methodology">Philosophical methods</a> include <a href="/wiki/Socratic_questioning" title="Socratic questioning">questioning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socratic_method" title="Socratic method">critical discussion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dialectic" title="Dialectic">rational argument</a>, and systematic presentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-justification_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-justification-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[i]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, <i>philosophy</i> encompassed all bodies of knowledge and a practitioner was known as a <i><a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-16">[15]</a></sup> From the time of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> to the 19th century, "<a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>" encompassed <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindberg20073_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindberg20073-17">[16]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a>'s 1687 <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Mathematical Principles of Natural Philosophy</a></i> later became classified as a book of physics. In the 19th century, the growth of modern <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">research universities</a> led academic philosophy and other disciplines to <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_philosophy" title="Contemporary philosophy">professionalize</a> and specialize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> Since then, various areas of investigation that were traditionally part of philosophy have become separate academic disciplines, and namely the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_sciences" title="Social sciences">social sciences</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>.
</p><p>Today, major subfields of academic philosophy include <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, which is concerned with the fundamental nature of <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">reality</a>; <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>, which studies the nature of <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a>; <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>, which is concerned with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Value_(ethics)" title="Value (ethics)">moral value</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, which studies the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rules_of_inference" title="Rules of inference">rules of inference</a> that allow one to derive <a href="/wiki/Consequent" title="Consequent">conclusions</a> from <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">true</a> <a href="/wiki/Premises" title="Premises">premises</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> Other notable subfields include <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_religion" title="Philosophy of religion">philosophy of religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">philosophy of science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">political philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_language" title="Philosophy of language">philosophy of language</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a>.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Definitions_of_philosophy" title="Definitions of philosophy">Definitions of philosophy</a></div>
<p>There is wide agreement that philosophy (from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">ancient Greek</a> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">φίλος</span></span>, phílos: "love"; and <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">σοφία</span></span>, sophía: "wisdom")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> is characterized by various general features: it is a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rational" title="Rational">rational</a> inquiry, it aims to be systematic, and it tends to critically reflect on its own methods and presuppositions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacmillanPhilosophy_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacmillanPhilosophy-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OxfordPhilosophy_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordPhilosophy-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe-25">[24]</a></sup> But approaches that go beyond such vague characterizations to give a more interesting or profound definition are usually controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OxfordPhilosophy_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordPhilosophy-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe-25">[24]</a></sup> Often, they are only accepted by theorists belonging to a certain <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_movement" title="Philosophical movement">philosophical movement</a> and are revisionistic in that many presumed parts of philosophy would not deserve the title "philosophy" if they were true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPMetaphilosophy_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEPMetaphilosophy-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard-27">[26]</a></sup> Before the modern age, the term was used in a very wide sense, which included the individual <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">sciences</a>, like <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, as its sub-disciplines, but the contemporary usage is more narrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baggini_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baggini-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some approaches argue that there is a set of essential features shared by all parts of philosophy while others see only weaker family resemblances or contend that it is merely an empty blanket term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MetzlerPhilosophie_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-MetzlerPhilosophie-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quine_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quine-31">[30]</a></sup> Some definitions characterize philosophy in relation to its method, like pure reasoning. Others focus more on its topic, for example, as the study of the biggest patterns of the world as a whole or as the attempt to answer the big questions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rescher_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rescher-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nuttall1_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nuttall1-33">[32]</a></sup> Both approaches have the problem that they are usually either too wide, by including non-philosophical disciplines, or too narrow, by excluding some philosophical sub-disciplines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard-27">[26]</a></sup> Many definitions of philosophy emphasize its intimate relation to science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe-25">[24]</a></sup> In this sense, philosophy is sometimes understood as a proper science in its own right. Some <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">naturalist</a> approaches, for example, see philosophy as an empirical yet very abstract science that is concerned with very wide-ranging empirical patterns instead of particular observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordQuine3_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordQuine3-34">[33]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(philosophy)" title="Phenomenology (philosophy)">phenomenologists</a>, on the other hand, characterize philosophy as the science of <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPMetaphilosophy_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEPMetaphilosophy-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gelan_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gelan-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ingarden_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ingarden-36">[35]</a></sup> Science-based definitions usually face the problem of explaining why philosophy in its long history has not made the type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_progress" title="Scientific progress">progress as seen in other sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> This problem is avoided by seeing philosophy as an immature or provisional science whose subdisciplines cease to be philosophy once they have fully developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MetzlerPhilosophie_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-MetzlerPhilosophie-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gelan_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gelan-35">[34]</a></sup> In this sense, philosophy is the midwife of the sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other definitions focus more on the contrast between science and philosophy. A common theme among many such definitions is that philosophy is concerned with <a href="/wiki/Meaning_(philosophy)" title="Meaning (philosophy)">meaning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Understanding" title="Understanding">understanding</a>, or the clarification of language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rescher_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rescher-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard_27-6"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard-27">[26]</a></sup> According to one view, philosophy is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conceptual_analysis" title="Conceptual analysis">conceptual analysis</a>, which involves finding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Necessary_and_sufficient_conditions" title="Necessary and sufficient conditions">necessary and sufficient conditions</a> for the application of concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nuttall1_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nuttall1-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard_27-7"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShafferConditions_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShafferConditions-39">[38]</a></sup> Another defines philosophy as a linguistic therapy that aims at dispelling misunderstandings to which humans are susceptible due to the confusing structure of <a href="/wiki/Natural_language" title="Natural language">natural language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPMetaphilosophy_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-IEPMetaphilosophy-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe_25-6"><a href="#cite_note-MeinerPhilosophiebegriffe-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordWittgenstein3.7_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordWittgenstein3.7-40">[39]</a></sup> One more approach holds that the main task of philosophy is to articulate the pre-ontological understanding of the world, which acts as a <a href="/wiki/Condition_of_possibility" title="Condition of possibility">condition of possibility</a> of <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard_27-8"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many other definitions of philosophy do not clearly fall into any of the aforementioned categories. An early approach already found in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">ancient Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_philosophy" title="Ancient Roman philosophy">Roman philosophy</a> is that philosophy is the spiritual practice of developing one's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> ability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banicki_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Banicki-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> This practice is an expression of the philosopher's love of wisdom and has the aim of improving one's <a href="/wiki/Well-being" title="Well-being">well-being</a> by leading a reflective life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grimm_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grimm-45">[44]</a></sup> A closely related approach identifies the development and articulation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Worldviews" title="Worldviews">worldviews</a> as the principal task of philosophy, i.e. to express how things on the grand scale hang together and which practical stance we should take towards them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard_27-9"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacmillanPhilosophy_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacmillanPhilosophy-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McIvor_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-McIvor-46">[45]</a></sup> Another definition characterizes philosophy as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thinking" title="Thinking">thinking</a> about thinking</i> in order to emphasize its reflective nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overgaard_27-10"><a href="#cite_note-Overgaard-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nuttall1_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nuttall1-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_overview">Historical overview</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <p>In one general sense, philosophy is associated with <a href="/wiki/Wisdom" title="Wisdom">wisdom</a>, intellectual culture, and a search for knowledge. In this sense, all cultures and literate societies ask philosophical questions, such as "how are we to live" and "what is the nature of reality". A broad and impartial conception of philosophy, then, finds a reasoned inquiry into such matters as <a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">reality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a>, and life in all world civilizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_philosophy">Western philosophy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western philosophy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristoteles_der_Stagirit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4084" data-file-width="2723" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Aristoteles_der_Stagirit.jpg/220px-Aristoteles_der_Stagirit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Aristoteles_der_Stagirit.jpg/330px-Aristoteles_der_Stagirit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Aristoteles_der_Stagirit.jpg/440px-Aristoteles_der_Stagirit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristoteles_der_Stagirit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (384–322 BCE), a major figure of ancient Greek philosophy, in Aristotle's Park, <a href="/wiki/Stagira" title="Stagira">Stagira</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western philosophy</a> is the philosophical tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>, dating back to <a href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic_philosophy" title="Pre-Socratic philosophy">pre-Socratic</a> thinkers who were active in 6th-century <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greece</a> (BCE), such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales" title="Thales">Thales</a> (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/circa#English" title="wikt:circa">c.</a><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 624</span> – <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/circa#English" title="wikt:circa">c.</a><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 545</span> BCE) and <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a> (<span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 570</span> – <span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 495</span> BCE) who practiced a 'love of wisdom' (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">philosophia</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-48">[47]</a></sup> and were also termed 'students of nature' (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">physiologoi</i></span>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western philosophy</a> can be divided into three eras:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Ancient</a> (<a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman</a>).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Medieval_philosophy" title="Medieval philosophy">Medieval philosophy</a> (referring to Christian European thought).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">Modern philosophy</a> (beginning in the 17th century).</li></ol>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_era">Ancient era</span></h4>
<p>While our knowledge of the ancient era begins with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales" title="Thales">Thales</a> in the 6th century BCE, little is known about the philosophers who came before <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> (commonly known as <a href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic_philosophy" title="Pre-Socratic philosophy">the pre-Socratics</a>). The ancient era was dominated by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Greek philosophical schools</a>. Most notable among the schools influenced by Socrates' teachings were <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, who founded the <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy" title="Platonic Academy">Platonic Academy</a>, and his student <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-process_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-process-49">[48]</a></sup> who founded the <a href="/wiki/Peripatetic_school" title="Peripatetic school">Peripatetic school</a>. Other ancient philosophical traditions influenced by Socrates included <a href="/wiki/Cynicism_(philosophy)" title="Cynicism (philosophy)">Cynicism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyrenaics" title="Cyrenaics">Cyrenaicism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoicism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic_Skepticism" title="Academic Skepticism">Academic Skepticism</a>. Two other traditions were influenced by Socrates' contemporary, <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a>: <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhonism" title="Pyrrhonism">Pyrrhonism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epicureanism" title="Epicureanism">Epicureanism</a>. Important topics covered by the Greeks included <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a> (with competing theories such as <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">atomism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monism</a>), <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>, the nature of the well-lived life (<i><a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">eudaimonia</a></i>), the <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">possibility of knowledge</a>, and the nature of reason (<a href="/wiki/Logos" title="Logos">logos</a>). With the rise of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_empire" title="Roman empire">Roman empire</a>, Greek philosophy was increasingly discussed in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca</a> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_philosophy" title="Roman philosophy">Roman philosophy</a>).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_era">Medieval era</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Medieval_philosophy" title="Medieval philosophy">Medieval philosophy</a> (5th–16th centuries) took place during the period following the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> and was dominated by the rise of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>; it hence reflects <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Christian" title="Judeo-Christian">Judeo-Christian</a> theological concerns while also retaining a continuity with Greco-Roman thought. Problems such as the existence and nature of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>, the nature of <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a> and reason, metaphysics, and the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">problem of evil</a> were discussed in this period. Some key medieval thinkers include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Augustine" title="St. Augustine">St. Augustine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boethius" title="Boethius">Boethius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Laon" title="Anselm of Laon">Anselm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">Roger Bacon</a>. Philosophy for these thinkers was viewed as an aid to <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ancilla theologiae</i></span>), and hence they sought to align their philosophy with their interpretation of sacred scripture. This period saw the development of <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholasticism</a>, a text critical method developed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_universities" title="Medieval universities">medieval universities</a> based on close reading and disputation on key texts. The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> period saw increasing focus on classic Greco-Roman thought and on a robust <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kant_doerstling2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="995" data-file-width="1575" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Kant_doerstling2.jpg/220px-Kant_doerstling2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Kant_doerstling2.jpg/330px-Kant_doerstling2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Kant_doerstling2.jpg/440px-Kant_doerstling2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kant_doerstling2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A painting of the influential modern philosopher <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> (in the blue coat) with his friends. Other figures include <a href="/wiki/Christian_Jakob_Kraus" title="Christian Jakob Kraus">Christian Jakob Kraus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_Hamann" title="Johann Georg Hamann">Johann Georg Hamann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Gottlieb_von_Hippel_the_Elder" title="Theodor Gottlieb von Hippel the Elder">Theodor Gottlieb von Hippel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Gottfried_Hagen" title="Karl Gottfried Hagen">Karl Gottfried Hagen</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Early_modern_philosophy" title="Early modern philosophy">Early modern philosophy</a> in the Western world begins with thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> (1596–1650).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diane_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-diane-50">[49]</a></sup> Following the rise of natural science, <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a> was concerned with developing a secular and rational foundation for knowledge and moved away from traditional structures of authority such as religion, scholastic thought and the Church. Major modern philosophers include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinoza" title="Spinoza">Spinoza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Berkeley" title="George Berkeley">Berkeley</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">Hume</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Kant</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/19th-century_philosophy" title="19th-century philosophy">19th-century philosophy</a> (sometimes called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_modern_philosophy" title="Late modern philosophy">late modern philosophy</a>) was influenced by the wider 18th-century movement termed "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Enlightenment" title="The Enlightenment">the Enlightenment</a>", and includes figures such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegel" title="Hegel">Hegel</a>, a key figure in <a href="/wiki/German_idealism" title="German idealism">German idealism</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kierkegaard" title="Kierkegaard">Kierkegaard</a>, who developed the foundations for <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">existentialism</a>; <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">Thomas Carlyle</a>, representative of the <a href="/wiki/Great_man_theory" title="Great man theory">great man theory</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nietzsche" title="Nietzsche">Nietzsche</a>, a famed anti-Christian; <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, who promoted <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a>; <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>, who developed the foundations for <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a>; and the American <a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a>. The 20th century saw the split between <a href="/wiki/Analytic_philosophy" title="Analytic philosophy">analytic philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Continental_philosophy" title="Continental philosophy">continental philosophy</a>, as well as philosophical trends such as <a href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(philosophy)" title="Phenomenology (philosophy)">phenomenology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">existentialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Logical_positivism" title="Logical positivism">logical positivism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">pragmatism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_turn" title="Linguistic turn">linguistic turn</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_philosophy" title="Contemporary philosophy">Contemporary philosophy</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Eastern_philosophy">Middle Eastern philosophy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_philosophy" title="Middle Eastern philosophy">Middle Eastern philosophy</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Islamic_philosophy">Pre-Islamic philosophy</span></h4>
<p>The regions of the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> are home to the earliest known philosophical wisdom literature.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Assyriology" title="Assyriology">assyriologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Marc_Van_de_Mieroop" title="Marc Van de Mieroop">Marc Van de Mieroop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian</a> philosophy was a highly developed system of thought with a unique approach to knowledge and a focus on writing, <a href="/wiki/Lexicography" title="Lexicography">lexicography</a>, divination, and law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> It was also a <a href="/wiki/Multilingualism" title="Multilingualism">bilingual</a> intellectual culture, based on <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">Sumerian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:History_of_civilization,_being_a_course_of_lectures_on_the_origin_and_development_of_the_main_institutions_of_mankind_(1887)_(14577423529).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="2202" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/History_of_civilization%2C_being_a_course_of_lectures_on_the_origin_and_development_of_the_main_institutions_of_mankind_%281887%29_%2814577423529%29.jpg/220px-History_of_civilization%2C_being_a_course_of_lectures_on_the_origin_and_development_of_the_main_institutions_of_mankind_%281887%29_%2814577423529%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/History_of_civilization%2C_being_a_course_of_lectures_on_the_origin_and_development_of_the_main_institutions_of_mankind_%281887%29_%2814577423529%29.jpg/330px-History_of_civilization%2C_being_a_course_of_lectures_on_the_origin_and_development_of_the_main_institutions_of_mankind_%281887%29_%2814577423529%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/History_of_civilization%2C_being_a_course_of_lectures_on_the_origin_and_development_of_the_main_institutions_of_mankind_%281887%29_%2814577423529%29.jpg/440px-History_of_civilization%2C_being_a_course_of_lectures_on_the_origin_and_development_of_the_main_institutions_of_mankind_%281887%29_%2814577423529%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:History_of_civilization,_being_a_course_of_lectures_on_the_origin_and_development_of_the_main_institutions_of_mankind_(1887)_(14577423529).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page of <i>The Maxims of Ptahhotep,</i> traditionally attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Vizier_(Ancient_Egypt)" title="Vizier (Ancient Egypt)">Vizier</a> <a href="/wiki/Ptahhotep" title="Ptahhotep">Ptahhotep</a> (c. 2375–2350 BCE)</div></div></div>
<p>Early <a href="/wiki/Wisdom_literature" title="Wisdom literature">Wisdom literature</a> from the Fertile Crescent was a genre that sought to instruct people on ethical action, practical living, and virtue through stories and proverbs. In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>, these texts were known as <i><a href="/wiki/Sebayt" title="Sebayt">sebayt</a></i> ('teachings'), and they are central to our understandings of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_philosophy" title="Ancient Egyptian philosophy">Ancient Egyptian philosophy</a>. The most well known of these texts is <i><a href="/wiki/The_Maxims_of_Ptahhotep" title="The Maxims of Ptahhotep">The Maxims of Ptahhotep</a>.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> Theology and cosmology were central concerns in Egyptian thought. Perhaps the earliest form of a <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> also emerged in Egypt, with the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Atenism" title="Atenism">Amarna theology (or Atenism)</a> of <a href="/wiki/Akhenaten" title="Akhenaten">Akhenaten</a> (14th century BCE), which held that the solar creation deity <a href="/wiki/Aten" title="Aten">Aten</a> was the only god. This has been described as a "monotheistic revolution" by <a href="/wiki/Egyptology" title="Egyptology">egyptologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Jan_Assmann" title="Jan Assmann">Jan Assmann</a>, though it also drew on previous developments in Egyptian thought, particularly the "New Solar Theology" based around <a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Amun-Ra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-55">[54]</a></sup> These theological developments also influenced the post-Amarna <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">Ramesside</a> theology, which retained a focus on a single creative solar deity (though without outright rejection of other gods, which are now seen as manifestations of the main solar deity). This period also saw the development of the concept of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_conception_of_the_soul" title="Ancient Egyptian conception of the soul"><i>ba</i></a> (soul) and its relation to god.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jewish_philosophy" title="Jewish philosophy">Jewish philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christian_philosophy" title="Christian philosophy">Christian philosophy</a> are religious-philosophical traditions that developed both in the Middle East and in Europe, which both share certain early Judaic texts (mainly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a>) and monotheistic beliefs. Jewish thinkers such as the <a href="/wiki/Geonim" title="Geonim">Geonim</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Talmudic_Academies_in_Babylonia" title="Talmudic Academies in Babylonia">Talmudic Academies in Babylonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a> engaged with Greek and Islamic philosophy. Later Jewish philosophy came under strong Western intellectual influences and includes the works of <a href="/wiki/Moses_Mendelssohn" title="Moses Mendelssohn">Moses Mendelssohn</a> who ushered in the <a href="/wiki/Haskalah" title="Haskalah">Haskalah</a> (the Jewish Enlightenment), <a href="/wiki/Jewish_existentialism" title="Jewish existentialism">Jewish existentialism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform Judaism</a>.
</p><p>The various traditions of <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnosticism</a>, which were influenced by both Greek and Abrahamic currents, originated around the first century and emphasized spiritual knowledge (<i><a href="/wiki/Gnosis" title="Gnosis">gnosis</a></i>).
</p><p>Pre-Islamic <a href="/wiki/Iranian_philosophy" title="Iranian philosophy">Iranian philosophy</a> begins with the work of <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroaster</a>, one of the first promoters of <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a> and of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualistic_cosmology" title="Dualistic cosmology">dualism</a> between good and evil. This dualistic cosmogony influenced later Iranian developments such as <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mazdakism" title="Mazdakism">Mazdakism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zurvanism" title="Zurvanism">Zurvanism</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_philosophy">Islamic philosophy</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic philosophy</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Avicenna_Portrait_on_Silver_Vase_-_Museum_at_BuAli_Sina_(Avicenna)_Mausoleum_-_Hamadan_-_Western_Iran_(7423560860).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Avicenna_Portrait_on_Silver_Vase_-_Museum_at_BuAli_Sina_%28Avicenna%29_Mausoleum_-_Hamadan_-_Western_Iran_%287423560860%29.jpg/220px-Avicenna_Portrait_on_Silver_Vase_-_Museum_at_BuAli_Sina_%28Avicenna%29_Mausoleum_-_Hamadan_-_Western_Iran_%287423560860%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Avicenna_Portrait_on_Silver_Vase_-_Museum_at_BuAli_Sina_%28Avicenna%29_Mausoleum_-_Hamadan_-_Western_Iran_%287423560860%29.jpg/330px-Avicenna_Portrait_on_Silver_Vase_-_Museum_at_BuAli_Sina_%28Avicenna%29_Mausoleum_-_Hamadan_-_Western_Iran_%287423560860%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Avicenna_Portrait_on_Silver_Vase_-_Museum_at_BuAli_Sina_%28Avicenna%29_Mausoleum_-_Hamadan_-_Western_Iran_%287423560860%29.jpg/440px-Avicenna_Portrait_on_Silver_Vase_-_Museum_at_BuAli_Sina_%28Avicenna%29_Mausoleum_-_Hamadan_-_Western_Iran_%287423560860%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Avicenna_Portrait_on_Silver_Vase_-_Museum_at_BuAli_Sina_(Avicenna)_Mausoleum_-_Hamadan_-_Western_Iran_(7423560860).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Iranian portrait of <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> on a Silver Vase. He was one of the most influential philosophers of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic philosophy</a> is the philosophical work originating in the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_culture" title="Islamic culture">Islamic tradition</a> and is mostly done in <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>. It draws from the religion of Islam as well as from Greco-Roman philosophy. After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Graeco-Arabic_translation_movement" title="Graeco-Arabic translation movement">translation movement</a> (mid-eighth to the late tenth century) resulted in the works of Greek philosophy becoming available in Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Early_Islamic_philosophy" title="Early Islamic philosophy">Early Islamic philosophy</a> developed the Greek philosophical traditions in new innovative directions. This intellectual work inaugurated what is known as the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>. The two main currents of early Islamic thought are <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Kalam</a>, which focuses on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_theology" title="Islamic theology">Islamic theology</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falsafa" title="Falsafa">Falsafa</a>, which was based on <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a>. The work of Aristotle was very influential among philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">Al-Kindi</a> (9th century), <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> (980 – June 1037), and <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> (12th century). Others such as <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a> were highly critical of the methods of the Islamic Aristotelians and saw their metaphysical ideas as heretical. Islamic thinkers like <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a> also developed a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>, experimental medicine, a theory of optics, and a legal philosophy. <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khaldun" title="Ibn Khaldun">Ibn Khaldun</a> was an influential thinker in <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_history" title="Philosophy of history">philosophy of history</a>.
</p><p>Islamic thought also deeply influenced European intellectual developments, especially through the commentaries of Averroes on Aristotle. The <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_the_Levant" title="Mongol invasions of the Levant">Mongol invasions</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">destruction of Baghdad</a> in 1258 are often seen as marking the end of the Golden Age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> Several schools of Islamic philosophy continued to flourish after the Golden Age, however, and include currents such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illuminationist_philosophy" title="Illuminationist philosophy">Illuminationist philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sufi_philosophy" title="Sufi philosophy">Sufi philosophy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transcendent_theosophy" title="Transcendent theosophy">Transcendent theosophy</a>.
</p><p>The 19th- and 20th-century <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a> saw the <i><a href="/wiki/Nahda" title="Nahda">Nahda</a></i> movement (literally meaning 'The Awakening'; also known as the 'Arab Renaissance'), which had a considerable influence on <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_Islamic_philosophy" title="Contemporary Islamic philosophy">contemporary Islamic philosophy</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_philosophy">Eastern philosophy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eastern_philosophy" title="Eastern philosophy">Eastern philosophy</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_philosophy">Indian philosophy</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Indian philosophy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7262" data-file-width="5146" decoding="async" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg/220px-Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg/330px-Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg/440px-Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Adi_Shankara" title="Adi Shankara">Adi Shankara</a> is one of the most frequently studied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Indian philosophy</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">darśana</i></span></span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'point of view', 'perspective')<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> refers to the diverse philosophical traditions that emerged since the ancient times on the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. Indian philosophical traditions share various key concepts and ideas, which are defined in different ways and accepted or rejected by the different traditions. These include concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma"><i>dhárma</i></a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">karma</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pramana" title="Pramana">pramāṇa</a>,</i> <i><a href="/wiki/Du%E1%B8%A5kha" title="Duḥkha">duḥkha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83s%C4%81ra" title="Saṃsāra">saṃsāra</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">mokṣa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup></i>
</p><p>Some of the earliest surviving Indian philosophical texts are the <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Vedic_period#Later_Vedic_period_(c._1000_–_c._600_BCE)" title="Vedic period">later Vedic period</a> (1000–500 BCE), which are considered to preserve the ideas of <a href="/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion" title="Historical Vedic religion">Brahmanism</a>. Indian philosophical traditions are commonly grouped according to their relationship to the Vedas and the ideas contained in them. <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> originated at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Vedic_period" title="Vedic period">Vedic period</a>, while the various traditions grouped under <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> mostly emerged after the Vedic period as independent traditions. Hindus generally classify Indian philosophical traditions as either orthodox (<a href="/wiki/%C4%80stika_and_n%C4%81stika" title="Āstika and nāstika"><i>āstika</i></a>) or heterodox (<i>nāstika</i>) depending on whether they accept the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> and the theories of <i><a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">brahman</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)"><i>ātman</i></a> found therein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The schools which align themselves with the thought of the Upanishads, the so-called "orthodox" or "<a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a>" traditions, are often classified into six <i><a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">darśanas</a></i> or philosophies:<a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Sānkhya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yoga_(philosophy)" title="Yoga (philosophy)">Yoga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nyaya" title="Nyaya">Nyāya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaisheshika" title="Vaisheshika">Vaisheshika</a>, <a href="/wiki/M%C4%ABm%C4%81%E1%B9%83s%C4%81" title="Mīmāṃsā">Mimāmsā</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedānta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>The doctrines of the Vedas and Upanishads were interpreted differently by these six schools of <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">Hindu philosophy</a>, with varying degrees of overlap. They represent a "collection of philosophical views that share a textual connection", according to Chadha (2015).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> They also reflect a tolerance for a diversity of philosophical interpretations within Hinduism while sharing the same foundation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sharma1990p1_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sharma1990p1-67">[ii]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hindu philosophers of the six orthodox schools developed systems of epistemology (<i><a href="/wiki/Pramana" title="Pramana">pramana</a></i>) and investigated topics such as metaphysics, ethics, psychology (<i><a href="/wiki/Gu%E1%B9%87a" title="Guṇa">guṇa</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Hermeneutics" title="Hermeneutics">hermeneutics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soteriology" title="Soteriology">soteriology</a> within the framework of the Vedic knowledge, while presenting a diverse collection of interpretations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frazierintrop2_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-frazierintrop2-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> The commonly named six orthodox schools were the competing philosophical traditions of what has been called the "Hindu synthesis" of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Hinduism" title="History of Hinduism">classical Hinduism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are also other schools of thought which are often seen as "Hindu", though not necessarily orthodox (since they may accept different scriptures as normative, such as the <a href="/wiki/Tantras_(Hinduism)" title="Tantras (Hinduism)">Shaiva Agamas and Tantras</a>), these include different schools of <a href="/wiki/Shaivism" title="Shaivism">Shavism</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Pashupata_Shaivism" title="Pashupata Shaivism">Pashupata</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shaiva_Siddhanta" title="Shaiva Siddhanta">Shaiva Siddhanta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kashmir_Shaivism" title="Kashmir Shaivism">non-dual tantric Shavism</a> (i.e. Trika, Kaula, etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1808" data-file-width="2463" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg/220px-Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg/330px-Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg/440px-Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The parable of the blind men and the elephant illustrates the important Jain doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Anekantavada" title="Anekantavada">anēkāntavāda</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The "Hindu" and "Orthodox" traditions are often contrasted with the "unorthodox" traditions (<i>nāstika,</i> literally "those who reject"), though this is a label that is not used by the "unorthodox" schools themselves. These traditions reject the Vedas as authoritative and often reject major concepts and ideas that are widely accepted by the orthodox schools (such as <i>Ātman</i>, <i>Brahman</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Ishvara" title="Ishvara"><i>Īśvara</i></a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bilimoriaastika_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-bilimoriaastika-76">[74]</a></sup> These unorthodox schools include Jainism (accepts <i>ātman</i> but rejects <i>Īśvara,</i> Vedas and <i>Brahman</i>), Buddhism (rejects all orthodox concepts except rebirth and karma), <a href="/wiki/Charvaka" title="Charvaka">Cārvāka</a> (materialists who reject even rebirth and karma) and <a href="/wiki/%C4%80j%C4%ABvika" title="Ājīvika">Ājīvika</a> (known for their doctrine of fate).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bilimoriaastika_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-bilimoriaastika-76">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bhattacarv_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-bhattacarv-77">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bronkhorst_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-bronkhorst-78">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[iii]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jain_philosophy" title="Jain philosophy">Jain philosophy</a> is one of the only two surviving "unorthodox" traditions (along with Buddhism). It generally accepts the concept of a permanent soul (<i><a href="/wiki/Jiva" title="Jiva">jiva</a></i>) as one of the five <i><a href="/wiki/%C4%80stika_and_n%C4%81stika" title="Āstika and nāstika">astikayas</a></i> (eternal, infinite categories that make up the substance of existence). The other four being <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma"><i>dhárma</i></a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Adharma" title="Adharma">adharma</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Akasha" title="Akasha">ākāśa</a></i> ('space'), and <i><a href="/wiki/Pudgala" title="Pudgala">pudgala</a></i> ('matter'). Jain thought holds that all existence is cyclic, eternal and uncreated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the most important elements of Jain philosophy are the <a href="/wiki/Karma_in_Jainism" title="Karma in Jainism">Jain theory of karma</a>, the doctrine of nonviolence (<a href="/wiki/Ahimsa_in_Jainism" title="Ahimsa in Jainism">ahiṃsā</a>) and the theory of "many-sidedness" or <a href="/wiki/Anekantavada" title="Anekantavada">Anēkāntavāda</a>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Tattvartha_Sutra" title="Tattvartha Sutra">Tattvartha Sutra</a></i> is the earliest known, most comprehensive and authoritative compilation of Jain philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhist_philosophy">Buddhist philosophy</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_philosophy" title="Buddhist philosophy">Buddhist philosophy</a></div>

codecs="vp9, opus"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monks_debating_at_Sera_monastery,_2013.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Monks debating at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sera_monastery" title="Sera monastery">Sera monastery</a>, Tibet, 2013. According to Jan Westerhoff, "public debates constituted the most important and most visible forms of philosophical exchange" in ancient Indian intellectual life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Buddhist philosophy begins with the thought of <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a> (<a href="/wiki/Floruit" title="Floruit">fl.</a> between 6th and 4th century BCE) and is preserved in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Buddhist_texts" title="Early Buddhist texts">early Buddhist texts</a>. It originated in the Indian region of <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a> and later spread to the rest of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>. In these regions, Buddhist thought developed into different philosophical traditions which used various languages (like <a href="/wiki/Classical_Tibetan" title="Classical Tibetan">Tibetan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pali" title="Pali">Pali</a>). As such, Buddhist philosophy is a <a href="/wiki/Transculturalism" title="Transculturalism">trans-cultural</a> and international phenomenon.
</p><p>The dominant Buddhist philosophical traditions in <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_Buddhism" title="East Asian Buddhism">East Asian</a> nations are mainly based on Indian <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> Buddhism. The <a href="/wiki/Therav%C4%81da_Abhidhamma" title="Theravāda Abhidhamma">philosophy of the Theravada</a> school is dominant in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asian</a> countries like <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>.
</p><p>Because <a href="/wiki/Avidy%C4%81_(Buddhism)" title="Avidyā (Buddhism)">ignorance</a> to the true nature of things is considered one of the roots of suffering (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dukkha" title="Dukkha">dukkha</a></i>), Buddhist philosophy is concerned with epistemology, metaphysics, ethics and psychology. Buddhist philosophical texts must also be understood within the context of <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_meditation" title="Buddhist meditation">meditative practices</a> which are supposed to bring about certain cognitive shifts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-:17-89">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 8">: 8 </span></sup> Key innovative concepts include the <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">Four Noble Truths</a> as an analysis of <i>dukkha</i>, <a href="/wiki/Impermanence" title="Impermanence"><i>anicca</i></a> (impermanence), and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatta" title="Anatta">anatta</a></i> (non-self).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[iv]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the death of the Buddha, various groups began to systematize his main teachings, eventually developing comprehensive philosophical systems termed <i><a href="/wiki/Abhidharma" title="Abhidharma">Abhidharma</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-:17-89">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 37">: 37 </span></sup> Following the Abhidharma schools, Indian <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Nagarjuna" title="Nagarjuna">Nagarjuna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vasubandhu" title="Vasubandhu">Vasubandhu</a> developed the theories of <i><a href="/wiki/%C5%9A%C5%ABnyat%C4%81" title="Śūnyatā">śūnyatā</a></i> ('emptiness of all phenomena') and <a href="/wiki/Yogachara#Vijñapti-mātra" title="Yogachara"><i>vijñapti-matra</i></a> ('appearance only'), a form of phenomenology or <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_idealism" title="Transcendental idealism">transcendental idealism</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Dign%C4%81ga" title="Dignāga">Dignāga</a> school of <i><a href="/wiki/Pramana" title="Pramana">pramāṇa</a></i> ('means of knowledge') promoted a sophisticated form of <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_logico-epistemology" title="Buddhist logico-epistemology">Buddhist epistemology</a>.
</p><p>There were numerous schools, sub-schools, and traditions of Buddhist philosophy in ancient and medieval India. According to Oxford professor of Buddhist philosophy <a href="/wiki/Jan_Westerhoff" title="Jan Westerhoff">Jan Westerhoff</a>, the major Indian schools from 300 BCE to 1000 CE were:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-:17-89">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: xxiv">: xxiv </span></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81s%C4%81%E1%B9%83ghika" title="Mahāsāṃghika">Mahāsāṃghika</a> tradition (now extinct), the <a href="/wiki/Sthavira_nik%C4%81ya" title="Sthavira nikāya">Sthavira</a> schools (such as <a href="/wiki/Sarvastivada" title="Sarvastivada">Sarvāstivāda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vibhajyav%C4%81da" title="Vibhajyavāda">Vibhajyavāda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pudgalavada" title="Pudgalavada">Pudgalavāda</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> schools. Many of these traditions were also studied in other regions, like Central Asia and China, having been brought there by Buddhist missionaries.
</p><p>After the disappearance of Buddhism from India, some of these philosophical traditions continued to develop in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhist" title="Tibetan Buddhist">Tibetan Buddhist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Asian_Buddhist" title="East Asian Buddhist">East Asian Buddhist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theravada_Buddhist" title="Theravada Buddhist">Theravada Buddhist</a> traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asian_philosophy">East Asian philosophy</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_philosophy" title="Korean philosophy">Korean philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_philosophy" title="Japanese philosophy">Japanese philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_philosophy" title="Vietnamese philosophy">Vietnamese philosophy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_philosophy" title="Eastern philosophy">Eastern philosophy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%27The_Three_Vinegar_Tasters%27_by_Kano_Isen%27in,_c._1802-1816,_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art,_6156.1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2152" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/%27The_Three_Vinegar_Tasters%27_by_Kano_Isen%27in%2C_c._1802-1816%2C_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art%2C_6156.1.JPG/300px-%27The_Three_Vinegar_Tasters%27_by_Kano_Isen%27in%2C_c._1802-1816%2C_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art%2C_6156.1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/%27The_Three_Vinegar_Tasters%27_by_Kano_Isen%27in%2C_c._1802-1816%2C_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art%2C_6156.1.JPG/450px-%27The_Three_Vinegar_Tasters%27_by_Kano_Isen%27in%2C_c._1802-1816%2C_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art%2C_6156.1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/%27The_Three_Vinegar_Tasters%27_by_Kano_Isen%27in%2C_c._1802-1816%2C_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art%2C_6156.1.JPG/600px-%27The_Three_Vinegar_Tasters%27_by_Kano_Isen%27in%2C_c._1802-1816%2C_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art%2C_6156.1.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%27The_Three_Vinegar_Tasters%27_by_Kano_Isen%27in,_c._1802-1816,_Honolulu_Museum_of_Art,_6156.1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Vinegar_tasters" title="Vinegar tasters">The Vinegar Tasters</a> (Japan, <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a>, 1802–1816) by Kanō Isen'in, depicting three prominent philosophical figures in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_philosophy" title="Eastern philosophy">East Asian thought</a>: <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E5%BB%B6%E5%AE%BE%E9%A6%86.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1328" data-file-width="1880" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/%E5%BB%B6%E5%AE%BE%E9%A6%86.JPG/220px-%E5%BB%B6%E5%AE%BE%E9%A6%86.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/%E5%BB%B6%E5%AE%BE%E9%A6%86.JPG/330px-%E5%BB%B6%E5%AE%BE%E9%A6%86.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/%E5%BB%B6%E5%AE%BE%E9%A6%86.JPG/440px-%E5%BB%B6%E5%AE%BE%E9%A6%86.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E5%BB%B6%E5%AE%BE%E9%A6%86.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of the Neo-Confucian scholar <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> at the <a href="/wiki/White_Deer_Grotto_Academy" title="White Deer Grotto Academy">White Deer Grotto Academy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lu" title="Mount Lu">Lushan Mountain</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="432" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/05/Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kitaro_Nishida" title="Kitaro Nishida">Kitaro Nishida</a>, considered the founder of the Kyoto School of philosophical thought, c. 1943</div></div></div>
<p>East Asian philosophical thought began in <a href="/wiki/History_of_China#Ancient_China" title="History of China">Ancient China</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosophy</a> begins during the <a href="/wiki/Western_Zhou" title="Western Zhou">Western Zhou</a> Dynasty and the following periods after its fall when the "<a href="/wiki/Hundred_Schools_of_Thought" title="Hundred Schools of Thought">Hundred Schools of Thought</a>" flourished (6th century to 221 BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pe_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-pe-95">[91]</a></sup> This period was characterized by significant intellectual and cultural developments and saw the rise of the major philosophical schools of China such as <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> (also known as Ruism), <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daoism" title="Daoism">Taoism</a> as well as numerous other less influential schools like <a href="/wiki/Mohism" title="Mohism">Mohism</a> and <a href="/wiki/School_of_Naturalists" title="School of Naturalists">Naturalism</a>. These philosophical traditions developed metaphysical, political and ethical theories such <a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">Tao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">Yin and yang</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ren_(Confucianism)" title="Ren (Confucianism)">Ren</a> and <a href="/wiki/Li_(Confucianism)" title="Li (Confucianism)">Li</a>.
</p><p>These schools of thought further developed during the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a> (206 BCE – 220 CE) and <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> (618–907 CE) eras, forming new philosophical movements like <i><a href="/wiki/Xuanxue" title="Xuanxue">Xuanxue</a></i> (also called <i>Neo-Taoism</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a>. Neo-Confucianism was a syncretic philosophy, which incorporated the ideas of different Chinese philosophical traditions, including Buddhism and Taoism. Neo-Confucianism came to dominate the education system during the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> (960–1297), and its ideas served as the philosophical basis of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_exams" title="Imperial exams">imperial exams</a> for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-officials" title="Scholar-officials">scholar official class</a>. Some of the most important Neo-Confucian thinkers are the Tang scholars <a href="/wiki/Han_Yu" title="Han Yu">Han Yu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Li_Ao_(philosopher)" title="Li Ao (philosopher)">Li Ao</a> as well as the Song thinkers <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Dunyi" title="Zhou Dunyi">Zhou Dunyi</a> (1017–1073) and <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> (1130–1200). Zhu Xi compiled the Confucian canon, which consists of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_Books" title="Four Books">Four Books</a> (the <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Learning" title="Great Learning">Great Learning</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_the_Mean" title="Doctrine of the Mean">Doctrine of the Mean</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Analects" title="Analects">Analects</a></i> of Confucius, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Mencius_(book)" title="Mencius (book)">Mencius</a></i>). The Ming scholar <a href="/wiki/Wang_Yangming" title="Wang Yangming">Wang Yangming</a> (1472–1529) is a later but important philosopher of this tradition as well.
</p><p>Buddhism began arriving in China during the Han Dynasty, through a <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road_transmission_of_Buddhism" title="Silk Road transmission of Buddhism">gradual Silk road transmission</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[92]</a></sup> and through native influences developed distinct Chinese forms (such as Chan/<a href="/wiki/Zen" title="Zen">Zen</a>) which spread throughout the <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_cultural_sphere" title="East Asian cultural sphere">East Asian cultural sphere</a>.
</p><p>Chinese culture was highly influential on the traditions of other East Asian states, and its philosophy directly influenced <a href="/wiki/Korean_philosophy" title="Korean philosophy">Korean philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_philosophy" title="Vietnamese philosophy">Vietnamese philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_philosophy" title="Japanese philosophy">Japanese philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[93]</a></sup> During later Chinese dynasties like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a> (1368–1644), as well as in the Korean <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseon_dynasty" title="Joseon dynasty">Joseon dynasty</a> (1392–1897), a resurgent <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a> led by thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/Wang_Yangming" title="Wang Yangming">Wang Yangming</a> (1472–1529) became the dominant school of thought and was promoted by the imperial state. In Japan, the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa shogunate</a> (1603–1867) was also strongly influenced by Confucian philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[94]</a></sup> Confucianism continues to influence the ideas and worldview of the nations of the <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_cultural_sphere" title="East Asian cultural sphere">Chinese cultural sphere</a> today.
</p><p>In the Modern era, Chinese thinkers incorporated ideas from Western philosophy. <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Marxist_philosophy" title="Chinese Marxist philosophy">Chinese Marxist philosophy</a> developed under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a>, while a Chinese pragmatism developed under <a href="/wiki/Hu_Shih" title="Hu Shih">Hu Shih</a>. The old traditional philosophies also began to reassert themselves in the 20th century.
