For example, <a href="/wiki/New_Confucianism" title="New Confucianism">New Confucianism</a>, led by figures such as <a href="/wiki/Xiong_Shili" title="Xiong Shili">Xiong Shili</a>, has become quite influential. Likewise, <a href="/wiki/Humanistic_Buddhism" title="Humanistic Buddhism">Humanistic Buddhism</a> is a recent modernist Buddhist movement.
</p><p>Modern Japanese thought meanwhile developed under strong Western influences such as the study of Western Sciences (<a href="/wiki/Rangaku" title="Rangaku">Rangaku</a>) and the modernist <a href="/wiki/Meirokusha" title="Meirokusha">Meirokusha</a> intellectual society, which drew from European enlightenment thought and promoted liberal reforms as well as Western philosophies like Liberalism and Utilitarianism. Another trend in modern Japanese philosophy was the "National Studies" (<a href="/wiki/Kokugaku" title="Kokugaku">Kokugaku</a>) tradition. This intellectual trend sought to study and promote ancient Japanese thought and culture. Kokugaku thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/Motoori_Norinaga" title="Motoori Norinaga">Motoori Norinaga</a> sought to return to a pure Japanese tradition which they called <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a> that they saw as untainted by foreign elements.
</p><p>During the 20th century, the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_School" title="Kyoto School">Kyoto School</a>, an influential and unique Japanese philosophical school, developed from Western <a href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(philosophy)" title="Phenomenology (philosophy)">phenomenology</a> and Medieval Japanese Buddhist philosophy such as that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dogen" title="Dogen">Dogen</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="African_philosophy">African philosophy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_philosophy" title="African philosophy">African philosophy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zera_Yacob.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="207" data-file-width="289" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Zera_Yacob.jpg/220px-Zera_Yacob.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a8/Zera_Yacob.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zera_Yacob.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Painting of Zera Yacob from <a href="/wiki/Claude_Sumner" title="Claude Sumner">Claude Sumner</a>'s <i>Classical Ethiopian Philosophy</i></div></div></div>
<p>African philosophy is philosophy produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_people" title="African people">African people</a>, philosophy that presents African worldviews, ideas and themes, or philosophy that uses distinct African philosophical methods. Modern African thought has been occupied with <a href="/wiki/Ethnophilosophy" title="Ethnophilosophy">Ethnophilosophy</a>, that is, defining the very meaning of African philosophy and its unique characteristics and what it means to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_people" title="African people">African</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 17th century, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_philosophy" title="Ethiopian philosophy">Ethiopian philosophy</a> developed a robust literary tradition as exemplified by <a href="/wiki/Zera_Yacob_(philosopher)" title="Zera Yacob (philosopher)">Zera Yacob</a>. Another early African philosopher was <a href="/wiki/Anton_Wilhelm_Amo" title="Anton Wilhelm Amo">Anton Wilhelm Amo</a> (c. 1703–1759) who became a respected philosopher in Germany. Distinct African philosophical ideas include <a href="/wiki/Ujamaa" title="Ujamaa">Ujamaa</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples_in_South_Africa" title="Bantu peoples in South Africa">Bantu</a> idea of <a href="/wiki/Bantu_Philosophy" title="Bantu Philosophy">'Force'</a>, <a href="/wiki/N%C3%A9gritude" title="Négritude">Négritude</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pan-Africanism" title="Pan-Africanism">Pan-Africanism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ubuntu_(philosophy)" title="Ubuntu (philosophy)">Ubuntu</a>. Contemporary African thought has also seen the development of Professional philosophy and of <a href="/wiki/Africana_philosophy" title="Africana philosophy">Africana philosophy</a>, the philosophical literature of the <a href="/wiki/African_diaspora" title="African diaspora">African diaspora</a> which includes currents such as <a href="/wiki/Black_existentialism" title="Black existentialism">black existentialism</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-Americans" title="African-Americans">African-Americans</a>. Some modern African thinkers have been influenced by <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, <a href="/wiki/African-American_literature" title="African-American literature">African-American literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Critical_theory" title="Critical theory">Critical theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Critical_race_theory" title="Critical race theory">Critical race theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Postcolonialism" title="Postcolonialism">Postcolonialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/African_feminism" title="African feminism">Feminism</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indigenous_American_philosophy">Indigenous American philosophy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_American_philosophy" title="Indigenous American philosophy">Indigenous American philosophy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tlamatini_observe_stars_-_Codex_Mendoza.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="292" data-file-width="279" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Tlamatini_observe_stars_-_Codex_Mendoza.jpg/220px-Tlamatini_observe_stars_-_Codex_Mendoza.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c9/Tlamatini_observe_stars_-_Codex_Mendoza.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tlamatini_observe_stars_-_Codex_Mendoza.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Tlamatini" title="Tlamatini">Tlamatini</a> (Aztec philosopher) observing the stars, from the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Mendoza" title="Codex Mendoza">Codex Mendoza</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Indigenous-American</a> philosophical thought consists of a wide variety of beliefs and traditions among different American cultures. Among some of <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">U.S. Native American</a> communities, there is a belief in a metaphysical principle called the '<a href="/wiki/Great_Spirit" title="Great Spirit">Great Spirit</a>' (<a href="/wiki/Siouan_languages" title="Siouan languages">Siouan</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Wakan_Tanka" title="Wakan Tanka">wakȟáŋ tȟáŋka</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Algonquian_languages" title="Algonquian languages">Algonquian</a>: <a href="/wiki/Gitche_Manitou" title="Gitche Manitou"><i>gitche manitou</i></a>). Another widely shared concept was that of <i><a href="/wiki/Orenda" title="Orenda">orenda</a></i> ('spiritual power'). According to Whiteley (1998), for the Native Americans, "mind is critically informed by transcendental experience (dreams, visions and so on) as well as by reason."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rep.routledge.com_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-rep.routledge.com-100">[96]</a></sup> The practices to access these transcendental experiences are termed <i><a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shamanism</a></i>. Another feature of the indigenous American worldviews was their extension of ethics to non-human animals and plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rep.routledge.com_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-rep.routledge.com-100">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aztec_philosophy" title="Aztec philosophy">Nahua philosophy</a> was an intellectual tradition developed by individuals called <i><a href="/wiki/Tlamatini" title="Tlamatini">tlamatini</a></i> ('those who know something')<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[98]</a></sup> and its ideas are preserved in various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec_codices" title="Aztec codices">Aztec codices</a> and fragmentary texts. Some of these philosophers are known by name, such as <a href="/wiki/Nezahualcoyotl_(tlatoani)" title="Nezahualcoyotl (tlatoani)">Nezahualcoyotl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aquiauhtzin" title="Aquiauhtzin">Aquiauhtzin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xayacamach" title="Xayacamach">Xayacamach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tochihuitzin_coyolchiuhqui" title="Tochihuitzin coyolchiuhqui">Tochihuitzin coyolchiuhqui</a> and Cuauhtencoztli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-103">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-104">[100]</a></sup> These authors were also poets and some of their work has survived in the original <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-103">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-104">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aztec philosophers developed theories of metaphysics, epistemology, values, and aesthetics. Aztec ethics was focused on seeking <i>tlamatiliztli</i> ('knowledge', 'wisdom') which was based on moderation and balance in all actions as in the <a href="/wiki/Nahuas" title="Nahuas">Nahua</a> proverb "the middle good is necessary."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iep.utm.edu_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-iep.utm.edu-105">[101]</a></sup> The Nahua worldview posited the concept of an ultimate universal energy or force called <i><a href="/wiki/%C5%8Cmete%C5%8Dtl" title="Ōmeteōtl">Ōmeteōtl</a></i> ('Dual Cosmic Energy') which sought a way to live in balance with a constantly changing, "slippery" world. The theory of <i><a href="/wiki/Teotl" title="Teotl">Teotl</a></i> can be seen as a form of <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">Pantheism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iep.utm.edu_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-iep.utm.edu-105">[101]</a></sup> According to James Maffie, Nahua metaphysics posited that teotl is "a single, vital, dynamic, vivifying, eternally self-generating and self-conceiving as well as self-regenerating and self-reconceiving sacred energy or force."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_104-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-104">[100]</a></sup> This force was seen as the all-encompassing life force of the universe and as the universe itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_104-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-104">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pachacuteckoricancha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1083" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Pachacuteckoricancha.jpg/220px-Pachacuteckoricancha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Pachacuteckoricancha.jpg/330px-Pachacuteckoricancha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Pachacuteckoricancha.jpg/440px-Pachacuteckoricancha.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pachacuteckoricancha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of <a href="/wiki/Pachacuti" title="Pachacuti">Pachacuti</a> worshipping <a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a> (god Sun) at <a href="/wiki/Coricancha" title="Coricancha">Coricancha</a>, in the 17th century second chronicles of <a href="/wiki/Mart%C3%ADn_de_Mur%C3%BAa" title="Martín de Murúa">Martín de Murúa</a>. Pachacuti was a major Incan ruler, author and poet.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inca_civilization" title="Inca civilization">Inca civilization</a> also had an elite class of philosopher-scholars termed the <i>amawtakuna</i> or <i>amautas</i> who were important in the <a href="/wiki/Inca_education" title="Inca education">Inca education</a> system as teachers of philosophy, theology, astronomy, poetry, law, music, morality and history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-106">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-107">[103]</a></sup> Young Inca nobles were educated in these disciplines at the state college of Yacha-huasi in <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">Cuzco</a>, where they also learned the art of the <a href="/wiki/Quipu" title="Quipu">quipu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-106">[102]</a></sup> Incan philosophy (as well as the broader category of Andean thought) held that the universe is animated by a single dynamic life force (sometimes termed <i>camaquen</i> or <i>camac</i>, as well as <i>upani</i> and <i>amaya</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-108">[104]</a></sup> This singular force also arises as a set of dual complementary yet opposite forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-108">[104]</a></sup> These "complementary opposites" are called <a href="/wiki/Yanantin" title="Yanantin">yanantin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yanantin#Masintin" title="Yanantin">masintin</a>. They are expressed as various polarities or dualities (such as male–female, dark–light, life and death, above and below) which interdependently contribute to the harmonious whole that is the universe through the process of reciprocity and mutual exchange called <i>ayni</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-:6-108">[104]</a></sup> The Inca worldview also included the belief in a <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">creator God</a> (<a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Viracocha</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-107">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Branches_of_philosophy">Branches of philosophy</span></h2>
<p>Philosophical questions can be grouped into various branches. These groupings allow philosophers to focus on a set of similar topics and interact with other thinkers who are interested in the same questions.
</p><p>These divisions are neither exhaustive nor mutually exclusive. (A philosopher might specialize in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kant" title="Kant">Kantian</a> epistemology, or <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Platonic</a> aesthetics, or modern political philosophy). Furthermore, these philosophical inquiries sometimes overlap with each other and with other inquiries such as science, religion or mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aesthetics">Aesthetics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">Aesthetics</a></div>
<p>Aesthetics is the "critical reflection on art, culture and <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[108]</a></sup> It addresses the nature of <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beauty" title="Beauty">beauty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taste_(sociology)" title="Taste (sociology)">taste</a>, enjoyment, emotional values, perception and the creation and appreciation of beauty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[109]</a></sup> It is more precisely defined as the study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Senses" title="Senses">sensory</a> or sensori-emotional values, sometimes called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judgment" title="Judgment">judgments</a> of <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">sentiment</a> and taste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[110]</a></sup> Its major divisions are art theory, <a href="/wiki/Literary_theory" title="Literary theory">literary theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Film_theory" title="Film theory">film theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_theory" title="Music theory">music theory</a>. An example from art theory is to discern the set of principles underlying the work of a particular artist or artistic movement such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cubist" title="Cubist">Cubist</a> aesthetic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics">Ethics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">Ethics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BeijingConfuciusTemple9.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/BeijingConfuciusTemple9.jpg/200px-BeijingConfuciusTemple9.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/BeijingConfuciusTemple9.jpg/300px-BeijingConfuciusTemple9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/BeijingConfuciusTemple9.jpg/400px-BeijingConfuciusTemple9.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BeijingConfuciusTemple9.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> <a href="/wiki/Guozijian_(Beijing)" title="Guozijian (Beijing)">imperial college</a> was an intellectual center for Confucian ethics and classics during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuan_Dynasty" title="Yuan Dynasty">Yuan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" title="Qing Dynasty">Qing</a> dynasties.</div></div></div>
<p>Ethics, also known as moral philosophy, studies what constitutes good and bad <a href="/wiki/Action_(philosophy)" title="Action (philosophy)">conduct</a>, right and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wrong" title="Wrong">wrong</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Values_(philosophy)" title="Values (philosophy)">values</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Good_and_evil" title="Good and evil">good and evil</a>. Its primary investigations include exploring how to live a good life and identifying standards of <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a>. It also includes investigating whether there <i>is</i> a best way to live or a universal moral standard, and if so, how we come to learn about it. The main branches of ethics are <a href="/wiki/Normative_ethics" title="Normative ethics">normative ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meta-ethics" title="Meta-ethics">meta-ethics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Applied_ethics" title="Applied ethics">applied ethics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEP_Ethics_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEP_Ethics-116">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>The three main views in ethics about what constitute moral actions are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEP_Ethics_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEP_Ethics-116">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Consequentialism" title="Consequentialism">Consequentialism</a>, which judges actions based on their consequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-117">[113]</a></sup> One such view is <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a>, which judges actions based on the net happiness (or pleasure) and/or lack of suffering (or pain) that they produce.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Deontology" title="Deontology">Deontology</a>, which judges actions based on whether they are in accordance with one's moral duty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-117">[113]</a></sup> In the standard form defended by <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>, deontology is concerned with whether a choice respects the moral agency of other people, regardless of its consequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto-117">[113]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Virtue_ethics" title="Virtue ethics">Virtue ethics</a>, which judges actions based on the moral character of the agent who performs them and whether they conform to what an ideally virtuous agent would do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_117-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto-117">[113]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemology">Epistemology</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">Epistemology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dignaga.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Dignaga.jpg/200px-Dignaga.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Dignaga.jpg/300px-Dignaga.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Dignaga.jpg/400px-Dignaga.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dignaga.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dignaga" title="Dignaga">Dignaga</a> founded a school of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist_logic" title="Buddhist logic">Buddhist epistemology and logic</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">Epistemology</a> is the branch of philosophy that studies <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-118">[114]</a></sup> Epistemologists examine putative sources of knowledge, including <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perceptual experience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>, <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Testimony#Philosophy" title="Testimony">testimony</a>. They also investigate questions about the nature of <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a>, <a href="/wiki/Justification_(epistemology)" title="Justification (epistemology)">justification</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">rationality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Epistemology_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Epistemology-119">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">Philosophical skepticism</a>, which raises doubts about some or all claims to knowledge, has been a topic of interest throughout the history of philosophy. It arose early in <a href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic_philosophy" title="Pre-Socratic philosophy">Pre-Socratic philosophy</a> and became formalized with <a href="/wiki/Pyrrho" title="Pyrrho">Pyrrho</a>, the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhonism" title="Pyrrhonism">earliest Western school of philosophical skepticism</a>. It features prominently in the works of modern philosophers <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> and has remained a central topic in contemporary epistemological debates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Epistemology_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Epistemology-119">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the most notable epistemological debates is between <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-120">[116]</a></sup> Empiricism places emphasis on observational evidence via sensory experience as the source of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-120">[116]</a></sup> Empiricism is associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_posteriori_knowledge" title="A posteriori knowledge">a posteriori knowledge</a>, which is obtained through experience (such as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">scientific knowledge</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-120">[116]</a></sup> Rationalism places emphasis on reason as a source of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_120-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-120">[116]</a></sup> Rationalism is associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_priori_knowledge" title="A priori knowledge">a priori knowledge</a>, which is independent of experience (such as <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>).
</p><p>One central debate in contemporary epistemology is about the conditions required for a <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a> to constitute knowledge, which might include <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Justification_(epistemology)" title="Justification (epistemology)">justification</a>. This debate was largely the result of attempts to solve the <a href="/wiki/Gettier_problem" title="Gettier problem">Gettier problem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Epistemology_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Epistemology-119">[115]</a></sup> Another common subject of contemporary debates is the <a href="/wiki/Regress_argument" title="Regress argument">regress problem</a>, which occurs when trying to offer proof or justification for any belief, statement, or proposition. The problem is that whatever the source of justification may be, that source must either be without justification (in which case it must be treated as an arbitrary <a href="/wiki/Foundationalism" title="Foundationalism">foundation</a> for belief), or it must have some further justification (in which case justification must either be the result of <a href="/wiki/Circular_reasoning" title="Circular reasoning">circular reasoning</a>, as in <a href="/wiki/Coherentism" title="Coherentism">coherentism</a>, or the result of an <a href="/wiki/Infinite_regress" title="Infinite regress">infinite regress</a>, as in <a href="/wiki/Infinitism" title="Infinitism">infinitism</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Epistemology_119-3"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Epistemology-119">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysics">Metaphysics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">Metaphysics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle,_Metaphysics,_Incunabulum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3589" data-file-width="2393" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Aristotle%2C_Metaphysics%2C_Incunabulum.jpg/200px-Aristotle%2C_Metaphysics%2C_Incunabulum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Aristotle%2C_Metaphysics%2C_Incunabulum.jpg/300px-Aristotle%2C_Metaphysics%2C_Incunabulum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Aristotle%2C_Metaphysics%2C_Incunabulum.jpg/400px-Aristotle%2C_Metaphysics%2C_Incunabulum.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle,_Metaphysics,_Incunabulum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The beginning of <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)">Aristotle's <i>Metaphysics</i></a> in an incunabulum decorated with hand-painted miniatures</div></div></div>
<p>Metaphysics is the study of the most general features of <a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">reality</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a>, <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object (philosophy)">objects</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">properties</a>, wholes and their parts, events, processes and <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causation</a> and the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_body" title="Human body">body</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[117]</a></sup> Metaphysics includes <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>, the study of the <a href="/wiki/World" title="World">world</a> in its entirety and <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontology</a>, the study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Being" title="Being">being</a>, along with the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_space_and_time" title="Philosophy of space and time">philosophy of space and time</a>.
</p><p>A major point of debate is between <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_realism" title="Philosophical realism">realism</a>, which holds that there are entities that exist independently of their mental perception, and <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealism</a>, which holds that reality is mentally constructed or otherwise immaterial. Metaphysics deals with the topic of <a href="/wiki/Personal_identity" title="Personal identity">identity</a>. <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">Essence</a> is the set of attributes that make an object what it fundamentally is and without which it loses its identity, while <a href="/wiki/Accident_(philosophy)" title="Accident (philosophy)">accident</a> is a property that the object has, without which the object can still retain its identity. <a href="/wiki/Particular" title="Particular">Particulars</a> are objects that are said to exist in space and time, as opposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abstract_object" title="Abstract object">abstract objects</a>, such as numbers, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universals" title="Universals">universals</a>, which are properties held by multiple particulars, such as redness or a gender. The type of existence, if any, of universals and abstract objects is an issue of debate.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Logic">Logic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">Logic</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">Logic</a> is the study of reasoning and argument.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">Deductive reasoning</a> is when, given certain premises, conclusions are <a href="/wiki/Logical_consequence" title="Logical consequence">unavoidably implied</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[118]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rules_of_inference" title="Rules of inference">Rules of inference</a> are used to infer conclusions such as, <a href="/wiki/Modus_ponens" title="Modus ponens">modus ponens</a>, where given "A” and "If A then B", then "B” must be concluded.
</p><p>Because sound reasoning is an essential element of all sciences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[119]</a></sup> social sciences and humanities disciplines, logic became a <a href="/wiki/Formal_science" title="Formal science">formal science</a>. Sub-fields include <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">mathematical logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_logic" title="Philosophical logic">philosophical logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computational_logic" title="Computational logic">computational logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Non-classical_logic" title="Non-classical logic">non-classical logics</a>. A major question in the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mathematics" title="Philosophy of mathematics">philosophy of mathematics</a> is whether mathematical entities are objective and discovered, called mathematical realism, or invented, called mathematical antirealism.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mind_and_language">Mind and language</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_language" title="Philosophy of language">Philosophy of language</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_language" title="Philosophy of language">Philosophy of language</a> explores the nature, origins, and use of language. <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">Philosophy of mind</a> explores the nature of the <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> and its relationship to the body, as typified by disputes between <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">dualism</a>. In recent years, this branch has become related to <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_of_science">Philosophy of science</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">Philosophy of science</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">philosophy of science</a> explores the foundations, methods, history, implications and purpose of science. Many of its subdivisions correspond to specific branches of science. For example, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_biology" title="Philosophy of biology">philosophy of biology</a> deals specifically with the metaphysical, epistemological and ethical issues in the biomedical and life sciences.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_philosophy">Political philosophy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">Political philosophy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Hobbes_(portrait).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1169" data-file-width="1109" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Thomas_Hobbes_%28portrait%29.jpg/220px-Thomas_Hobbes_%28portrait%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Thomas_Hobbes_%28portrait%29.jpg/330px-Thomas_Hobbes_%28portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Thomas_Hobbes_%28portrait%29.jpg/440px-Thomas_Hobbes_%28portrait%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Hobbes_(portrait).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, best known for his 1651 book <i><a href="/wiki/Leviathan_(Hobbes_book)" title="Leviathan (Hobbes book)">Leviathan</a></i>, which expounded an influential formulation of <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a> theory</div></div></div>
<p>Political philosophy is the study of <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a> and the relationship of individuals (or families and clans) to communities including the <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a>. It includes questions about justice, law, property and the rights and obligations of the citizen. Political philosophy, ethics, and aesthetics are traditionally linked subjects, under the general heading of <a href="/wiki/Value_theory" title="Value theory">value theory</a> as they involve a normative or evaluative aspect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_of_religion">Philosophy of religion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_religion" title="Philosophy of religion">Philosophy of religion</a></div>
<p>Philosophy of religion deals with questions that involve <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> and religious ideas from a philosophically neutral perspective (as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> which begins from religious convictions).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[121]</a></sup> Traditionally, religious questions were not seen as a separate field from philosophy proper, and the idea of a separate field only arose in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[v]</a></sup>
</p><p>Issues include the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a>, the relationship between reason and <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a>, questions of <a href="/wiki/Religious_epistemology" title="Religious epistemology">religious epistemology</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Relationship_between_religion_and_science" title="Relationship between religion and science">relationship between religion and science</a>, how to interpret <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_experiences" title="Religious experiences">religious experiences</a>, questions about the possibility of an <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_religious_language" title="Problem of religious language">problem of religious language</a> and the existence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Souls" title="Souls">souls</a> and responses to <a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">religious pluralism</a> and diversity.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphilosophy">Metaphilosophy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metaphilosophy" title="Metaphilosophy">Metaphilosophy</a></div>
<p>Metaphilosophy explores the aims, boundaries and methods of philosophy. It is debated as to whether metaphilosophy is a subject that comes prior to philosophy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[122]</a></sup> or whether it is inherently part of philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_subdivisions">Other subdivisions</span></h3>
<p>In section thirteen of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lives_and_Opinions_of_Eminent_Philosophers" title="Lives and Opinions of Eminent Philosophers"><i>Lives and Opinions of the Eminent Philosophers</i></a>, the oldest surviving history of philosophy (3rd century), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diogenes_La%C3%ABrtius" title="Diogenes Laërtius">Diogenes Laërtius</a> presents a three-part division of ancient Greek philosophical inquiry:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Natural philosophy</a> (i.e. physics, from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <i lang="el">ta physika</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'things having to do with <i>physis</i> [nature]') was the study of the constitution and processes of transformation in the physical world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[125]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">Moral philosophy</a> (i.e. ethics, from <span title="Greek-language text"><i lang="el">êthika</i></span>, 'having to do with character, disposition, manners') was the study of goodness, right and wrong, justice and virtue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[126]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">Metaphysical philosophy</a> (i.e. logic, from <span title="Greek-language text"><i lang="el">logikós</i></span>, 'of or pertaining to reason or speech') was the study of <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>, causation, <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>, <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Universal_(metaphysics)" title="Universal (metaphysics)">forms</a>, and other abstract objects. (<span title="Greek-language text"><i lang="el">meta ta physika</i></span>, 'after the <i><a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)">Physics</a></i>')</li></ul>
<p>In <i>Against the Logicians</i> the <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhonism" title="Pyrrhonism">Pyrrhonist</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Sextus_Empiricus" title="Sextus Empiricus">Sextus Empiricus</a> detailed the variety of ways in which the ancient Greek philosophers had divided philosophy, noting that this three-part division was agreed to by Plato, Aristotle, Xenocrates, and the Stoics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[127]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Academic_skepticism" title="Academic skepticism">Academic Skeptic</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> also followed this three-part division.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>This division is not obsolete, but has changed: <i>natural philosophy</i> has split into the various natural sciences, especially physics, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>; <i>moral philosophy</i> has birthed the <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social sciences</a>, while still including <a href="/wiki/Value_theory" title="Value theory">value theory</a> (e.g. ethics, <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">political philosophy</a>, etc.); and <i>metaphysical philosophy</i> has given way to formal sciences such as logic, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">philosophy of science</a>, while still including epistemology, cosmology, etc. For example, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Mathematical Principles of Natural Philosophy</a></i> (1687), since classified as a book of physics, uses the term <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a></i> as it was understood at the time, encompassing disciplines such as astronomy, <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> that later became associated with <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">the sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindberg20073_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindberg20073-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods_of_philosophy">Methods of philosophy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_methodology" title="Philosophical methodology">Philosophical methodology</a></div>
<p>Methods of philosophy are ways of conducting philosophical inquiry. They include techniques for arriving at philosophical knowledge and justifying philosophical claims as well as principles used for choosing between competing theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKeon_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKeon-134">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OvergaardMethodology_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-OvergaardMethodology-135">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeinerMethode_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-MeinerMethode-136">[131]</a></sup> A great variety of methods has been employed throughout the history of philosophy. Many of them differ significantly from the methods used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_sciences" title="Natural sciences">natural sciences</a> in that they do not use experimental data obtained through measuring equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DalyIntroduction_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-DalyIntroduction-137">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VeryShortIntro_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-VeryShortIntro-138">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ichikawa_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ichikawa-139">[134]</a></sup> The choice of one's method usually has important implications both for how philosophical theories are constructed and for the arguments cited for or against them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OvergaardMethodology_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-OvergaardMethodology-135">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cappelen_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cappelen-141">[136]</a></sup> This choice is often guided by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epistemological" title="Epistemological">epistemological</a> considerations about what constitutes philosophical <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a>, how much support it offers, and how to acquire it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DalyIntroduction_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-DalyIntroduction-137">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OvergaardMethodology_135-2"><a href="#cite_note-OvergaardMethodology-135">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dever_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dever-142">[137]</a></sup> Various disagreements on the level of philosophical theories have their source in methodological disagreements and the discovery of new methods has often had important consequences both for how philosophers conduct their research and for what claims they defend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DalyHandbook_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-DalyHandbook-143">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeinerMethode_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-MeinerMethode-136">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OvergaardMethodology_135-3"><a href="#cite_note-OvergaardMethodology-135">[130]</a></sup> Some philosophers engage in most of their theorizing using one particular method while others employ a wider range of methods based on which one fits the specific problem investigated best.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VeryShortIntro_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-VeryShortIntro-138">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methodological_skepticism" title="Methodological skepticism">Methodological skepticism</a> is a prominent method of philosophy. It aims to arrive at absolutely certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_principles" title="First principles">first principles</a> by using systematic <a href="/wiki/Doubt" title="Doubt">doubt</a> to determine which principles of philosophy are indubitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[140]</a></sup> The geometrical method tries to build a comprehensive philosophical system based on a small set of such axioms. It does so with the help of <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deductive reasoning</a> to expand the certainty of its axioms to the system as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(philosophy)" title="Phenomenology (philosophy)">Phenomenologists</a> seek certain knowledge about the realm of appearances. They do so by suspending their judgments about the external world in order to focus on how things appear independent of their underlying reality, a technique known as <a href="/wiki/Epoch%C3%A9" title="Epoché">epoché</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPReduction_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEPReduction-148">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeinerMethode_136-2"><a href="#cite_note-MeinerMethode-136">[131]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conceptual_analysis" title="Conceptual analysis">Conceptual analysis</a> is a well-known method in <a href="/wiki/Analytic_philosophy" title="Analytic philosophy">analytic philosophy</a>. It aims to clarify the meaning of concepts by analyzing them into their fundamental constituents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eder_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eder-149">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShafferConditions_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-ShafferConditions-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacmillanPhilosophy_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacmillanPhilosophy-23">[22]</a></sup> Another method often employed in analytic philosophy is based on <a href="/wiki/Common_sense" title="Common sense">common sense</a>. It starts with commonly accepted <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a> and tries to draw interesting conclusions from them, which it often employs in a negative sense to criticize philosophical theories that are too far removed from how the average person sees the issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ichikawa_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ichikawa-139">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaCommon_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaCommon-151">[146]</a></sup> It is very similar to how <a href="/wiki/Ordinary_language_philosophy" title="Ordinary language philosophy">ordinary language philosophy</a> tackles philosophical questions by investigating how <a href="/wiki/Natural_language" title="Natural language">ordinary language</a> is used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeinerMethode_136-3"><a href="#cite_note-MeinerMethode-136">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPOrdinary_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEPOrdinary-152">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Various methods in philosophy give particular importance to <a href="/wiki/Intuition" title="Intuition">intuitions</a>, i.e. non-inferential impressions about the correctness of specific claims or general principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DalyHandbook_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-DalyHandbook-143">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[149]</a></sup> For example, they play an important role in <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">thought experiments</a>, which employ <a href="/wiki/Counterfactual_thinking" title="Counterfactual thinking">counterfactual thinking</a> to evaluate the possible consequences of an imagined situation. These anticipated consequences can then be used to confirm or refute philosophical theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-155">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goffi_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goffi-156">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eder_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eder-149">[144]</a></sup> The method of <a href="/wiki/Reflective_equilibrium" title="Reflective equilibrium">reflective equilibrium</a> also employs intuitions. It seeks to form a <a href="/wiki/Coherentism" title="Coherentism">coherent</a> position on a certain issue by examining all the relevant beliefs and intuitions, some of which often have to be deemphasized or reformulated in order to arrive at a coherent perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DalyHandbook_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-DalyHandbook-143">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEquilibrium_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEquilibrium-157">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Little_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Little-158">[153]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pragmatists" title="Pragmatists">Pragmatists</a> stress the significance of concrete practical consequences for assessing whether a philosophical theory is true or false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPPragmatism_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEPPragmatism-159">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bawden_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bawden-160">[155]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Experimental_philosophy" title="Experimental philosophy">Experimental philosophy</a> is of rather recent origin. Its methods differ from most other methods of philosophy in that it tries to answer philosophical questions by gathering empirical data in ways similar to <a href="/wiki/Social_psychology" title="Social psychology">social psychology</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive_sciences" title="Cognitive sciences">cognitive sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordExperimental_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordExperimental-161">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plakias_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plakias-162">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophical_progress">Philosophical progress</span></h3>
<p>Many philosophical debates that began in ancient times are still debated today. British philosopher <a href="/wiki/Colin_McGinn" title="Colin McGinn">Colin McGinn</a> claims that no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophical_progress" title="Philosophical progress">philosophical progress</a> has occurred during that interval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[158]</a></sup> Australian philosopher <a href="/wiki/David_Chalmers" title="David Chalmers">David Chalmers</a>, by contrast, sees progress in philosophy similar to that in science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[159]</a></sup> Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Talbot_Brewer" title="Talbot Brewer">Talbot Brewer</a>, professor of philosophy at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a>, argues that "progress" is the wrong standard by which to judge philosophical activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applied_and_professional_philosophy">Applied and professional philosophy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2473" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg/170px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg/255px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg/340px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">Martin Luther King Jr.</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_philosophy#Outside_the_profession" title="Contemporary philosophy">Contemporary philosophy § Outside the profession</a></div>
<p>Some of those who study philosophy become professional philosophers, typically by working as <a href="/wiki/Professor" title="Professor">professors</a> who teach, research and write in academic institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[161]</a></sup> However, most students of academic philosophy later contribute to law, journalism, religion, sciences, politics, business, or various arts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whystudyphilosophy.com_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-whystudyphilosophy.com-167">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[163]</a></sup> For example, public figures who have degrees in philosophy include <a href="/wiki/Comedian" title="Comedian">comedians</a> <a href="/wiki/Steve_Martin" title="Steve Martin">Steve Martin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ricky_Gervais" title="Ricky Gervais">Ricky Gervais</a>, filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Terrence_Malick" title="Terrence Malick">Terrence Malick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul II</a>, Wikipedia co-founder <a href="/wiki/Larry_Sanger" title="Larry Sanger">Larry Sanger</a>, technology entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/Peter_Thiel" title="Peter Thiel">Peter Thiel</a>, U.S. Supreme Court Justice <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Breyer" title="Stephen Breyer">Stephen Breyer</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Jeopardy!" title="Jeopardy!">Jeopardy!</a></i> host <a href="/wiki/Alex_Trebek" title="Alex Trebek">Alex Trebek</a>, and US vice presidential candidate <a href="/wiki/Carly_Fiorina" title="Carly Fiorina">Carly Fiorina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[165]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Curtis_White_(author)" title="Curtis White (author)">Curtis White</a> has argued that philosophical tools are essential to humanities, sciences and social sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent efforts to avail the general public to the work and relevance of philosophers include the million-dollar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berggruen_Prize_for_Philosophy" title="Berggruen Prize for Philosophy">Berggruen Prize</a>, first awarded to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Taylor_(philosopher)" title="Charles Taylor (philosopher)">Charles Taylor</a> in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[167]</a></sup> Some philosophers argue that this professionalization has negatively affected the discipline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women_in_philosophy">Women in philosophy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_philosophy" title="Women in philosophy">Women in philosophy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mary_Wollstonecraft_by_John_Opie_(c._1797).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1244" data-file-width="1020" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Mary_Wollstonecraft_by_John_Opie_%28c._1797%29.jpg/180px-Mary_Wollstonecraft_by_John_Opie_%28c._1797%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Mary_Wollstonecraft_by_John_Opie_%28c._1797%29.jpg/270px-Mary_Wollstonecraft_by_John_Opie_%28c._1797%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Mary_Wollstonecraft_by_John_Opie_%28c._1797%29.jpg/361px-Mary_Wollstonecraft_by_John_Opie_%28c._1797%29.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mary_Wollstonecraft_by_John_Opie_(c._1797).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mary_Wollstonecraft" title="Mary Wollstonecraft">Mary Wollstonecraft</a> (1759–1797) was an English writer and philosopher.</div></div></div>
<p>Although men have generally dominated philosophical discourse, women philosophers have engaged in the discipline throughout history. <a href="/wiki/Ancient_philosophy" title="Ancient philosophy">Ancient</a> examples include <a href="/wiki/Hipparchia_of_Maroneia" title="Hipparchia of Maroneia">Hipparchia of Maroneia</a> (active <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 325 BCE</span>) and <a href="/wiki/Arete_of_Cyrene" title="Arete of Cyrene">Arete of Cyrene</a> (active 5th–4th centuries BCE). Some women philosophers were accepted during the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_philosophy" title="Medieval philosophy">medieval</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern</a> eras, but none became part of the <a href="/wiki/Western_canon" title="Western canon">Western canon</a> until the <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_philosophy" title="Contemporary philosophy">20th and 21st century</a>, when many suggest that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G.E.M._Anscombe" title="G.E.M. Anscombe">G.E.M. Anscombe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hannah_Arendt" title="Hannah Arendt">Hannah Arendt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simone_de_Beauvoir" title="Simone de Beauvoir">Simone de Beauvoir</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Susanne_Langer" title="Susanne Langer">Susanne Langer</a> entered the canon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duran,_Jane_2005_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duran,_Jane_2005-174">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-read.hipporeads.com_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-read.hipporeads.com-175">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1800s, some colleges and universities in the UK and the US began <a href="/wiki/Mixed-sex_education#Higher-education_institutions" title="Mixed-sex education">admitting women</a>, producing more female academics. Nevertheless, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Education" title="U.S. Department of Education">U.S. Department of Education</a> reports from the 1990s indicate that few women ended up in philosophy and that philosophy is one of the least gender-proportionate fields in the <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a>, with women making up somewhere between 17% and 30% of philosophy faculty according to some studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salary,_Promotion_1997_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salary,_Promotion_1997-177">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_philosophy" title="Outline of philosophy">Outline of philosophy</a></div>

<li><span><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="326" decoding="async" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/18px-Socrates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/27px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/36px-Socrates.png 2x" width="18"/></span><span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy portal</a></span></li></ul></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width reflist-lower-roman" style="column-width: 35em;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Quinton, Anthony. 1995. "The Ethics of Philosophical Practice." P. 666 in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Oxford_Companion_to_Philosophy" title="The Oxford Companion to Philosophy">The Oxford Companion to Philosophy</a></i>, edited by <a href="/wiki/Ted_Honderich" title="Ted Honderich">T. Honderich</a>. New York: <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">Oxford University Press</a>. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1067248974">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit;word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation q{quotes:"\"""\"""'""'"}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-free a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-free a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/Lock-green.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .id-lock-registration a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-registration a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Lock-gray-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-subscription a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .cs1-ws-icon a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg")right 0.1em center/12px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .cs1-code{color:inherit;background:inherit;border:none;padding:inherit}.mw-parser-output .cs1-hidden-error{display:none;color:#d33}.mw-parser-output .cs1-visible-error{color:#d33}.mw-parser-output .cs1-maint{display:none;color:#3a3;margin-left:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-format{font-size:95%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right{padding-right:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .citation .mw-selflink{font-weight:inherit}</style><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-866132-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-866132-0">978-0-19-866132-0</a>.

"Philosophy is rationally critical thinking, of a more or less systematic kind about the general nature of the world (metaphysics or theory of existence), the justification of belief (epistemology or theory of knowledge), and the conduct of life (ethics or theory of value). Each of the three elements in this list has a non-philosophical counterpart, from which it is distinguished by its explicitly rational and critical way of proceeding and by its systematic nature. Everyone has some general conception of the nature of the world in which they live and of their place in it. Metaphysics replaces the unargued assumptions embodied in such a conception with a rational and organized body of beliefs about the world as a whole. Everyone has occasion to doubt and question beliefs, their own or those of others, with more or less success and without any theory of what they are doing. Epistemology seeks by argument to make explicit the rules of correct belief formation. Everyone governs their conduct by directing it to desired or valued ends. Ethics, or moral philosophy, in its most inclusive sense, seeks to articulate, in rationally systematic form, the rules or principles involved." (p. 666).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Sharma1990p1-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Sharma1990p1_67-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFSharma1990">Sharma, Arvind (1990). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=jKewCwAAQBAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>A Hindu Perspective on the Philosophy of Religion</i></a>. Palgrave Macmillan. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-349-20797-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-349-20797-8"><bdi>978-1-349-20797-8</bdi></a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20200112222617/https://books.google.com/books?id=jKewCwAAQBAJ" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 12 January 2020<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">11 November</span> 2018</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+Hindu+Perspective+on+the+Philosophy+of+Religion&amp;rft.pub=Palgrave+Macmillan&amp;rft.date=1990&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-349-20797-8&amp;rft.aulast=Sharma&amp;rft.aufirst=Arvind&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DjKewCwAAQBAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APhilosophy"></span>

"The attitude towards the existence of God varies within the Hindu religious tradition. This may not be entirely unexpected given the tolerance for doctrinal diversity for which the tradition is known. Thus of the six orthodox systems of Hindu philosophy, only three address the question in some detail. These are the schools of thought known as Nyaya, Yoga and the theistic forms of Vedanta." (pp. 1–2).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wynne, Alexander. 2011. "The ātman and its negation." <i>Journal of the International Association of Buddhist Studies</i> 33(1–2):103–05. "The denial that a human being possesses a "self" or "soul" is probably the most famous Buddhist teaching. It is certainly its most distinct, as has been pointed out by <a href="/wiki/Gunapala_Piyasena_Malalasekera" title="Gunapala Piyasena Malalasekera">G.P. Malalasekera</a>: 'In its denial of any real permanent Soul or Self, Buddhism stands alone.' A similar modern Sinhalese perspective has been expressed by <a href="/wiki/Walpola_Rahula_Thero" title="Walpola Rahula Thero">Walpola Rahula</a>: 'Buddhism stands unique in the history of human thought in denying the existence of such a Soul, Self or Ātman.' The 'no Self' or 'no soul' doctrine (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa">anātman</span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pali_language" title="Pali language">Pali</a>: <span lang="pi">anattan</span>) is particularly notable for its widespread acceptance and historical endurance. It was a standard belief of virtually all the ancient schools of Indian Buddhism (the notable exception being the Pudgalavādins), and has persisted without change into the modern era.… [B]oth views are mirrored by the modern Theravādin perspective of <a href="/wiki/Mahasi_Sayadaw" title="Mahasi Sayadaw">Mahasi Sayadaw</a> that 'there is no person or soul' and the modern Mahāyāna view of the fourteenth Dalai Lama that '[t]he Buddha taught that…our belief in an independent self is the root cause of all suffering.'</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFGombrich,_Richard2006">Gombrich, Richard (2006). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=jZyJAgAAQBAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>Theravada Buddhism</i></a>. Routledge. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-134-90352-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-134-90352-8"><bdi>978-1-134-90352-8</bdi></a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190816142222/https://books.google.com/books?id=jZyJAgAAQBAJ" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 16 August 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">10 November</span> 2018</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Theravada+Buddhism&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-134-90352-8&amp;rft.au=Gombrich%2C+Richard&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DjZyJAgAAQBAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APhilosophy"></span> "All phenomenal existence [in Buddhism] is said to have three interlocking characteristics: impermanence, suffering and lack of soul or essence." (p. 47).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wainwright, William J. 2005. "Introduction." Pp. 3–11 in <i><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.ca/books?id=GbcuCf9TlDgC" rel="nofollow">The Oxford Handbook of Philosophy of Religion</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20200806100707/https://books.google.ca/books?id=GbcuCf9TlDgC" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 6 August 2020 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></i>, edited by W. J. Wainwright. New York: Oxford University Press.

"The expression “philosophy of religion” did not come into general use until the nineteenth century, when it was employed to refer to the articulation and criticism of humanity's religious consciousness and its cultural expressions in thought, language, feeling, and practice." (<i><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=GbcuCf9TlDgC&amp;pg=PA3" rel="nofollow">Oxford Handbook</a></i>, p. 3, at <a href="/wiki/Google_Books" title="Google Books">Google Books</a>).</span>
</li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFCicero1877">Cicero, Marcus Tullius (1877). <a class="external text" href="https://www.gutenberg.org/files/14988/14988-h/14988-h.htm" rel="nofollow"><i>Tusculan Disputations</i></a>. New York: Harper &amp; Brothers. p. 166. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210518014748/https://www.gutenberg.org/files/14988/14988-h/14988-h.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 18 May 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">12 June</span> 2021</span>. <q>From whence all who occupied themselves in the contemplation of nature were both considered and called wise men; and that name of theirs continued to the age of Pythagoras, who is reported to have gone to Phlius, as we find it stated by Heraclides Ponticus, a very learned man, and a pupil of Plato, and to have discoursed very learnedly and copiously on certain subjects with Leon, prince of the Phliasii; and when Leon, admiring his ingenuity and eloquence, asked him what art he particularly professed, his answer was, that he was acquainted with no art, but that he was a philosopher. Leon, surprised at the novelty of the name, inquired what he meant by the name of philosopher, and in what philosophers differed from other men; on which Pythagoras replied, "That the life of man seemed to him to resemble those games which were celebrated with the 166greatest possible variety of sports and the general concourse of all Greece. For as in those games there were some persons whose object was glory and the honor of a crown, to be attained by the performance of bodily exercises, so others were led thither by the gain of buying and selling, and mere views of profit; but there was likewise one class of persons, and they were by far the best, whose aim was neither applause nor profit, but who came merely as spectators through curiosity, to observe what was done, and to see in what manner things were carried on there. And thus, said he, we come from another life and nature unto this one, just as men come out of some other city, to some much frequented mart; some being slaves to glory, others to money; and there are some few who, taking no account of anything else, earnestly look into the nature of things; and these men call themselves studious of wisdom, that is, philosophers: and as there it is the most reputable occupation of all to be a looker-on without making any acquisition, so in life, the contemplating things, and acquainting one’s self with them, greatly exceeds every other pursuit of life.”</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Tusculan+Disputations&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pages=166&amp;rft.pub=Harper+%26+Brothers&amp;rft.date=1877&amp;rft.aulast=Cicero&amp;rft.aufirst=Marcus+Tullius&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gutenberg.org%2Ffiles%2F14988%2F14988-h%2F14988-h.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APhilosophy"></span></span>
</li>

George Banta Publishing Company.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Pythagorean+Background+of+the+theory+of+Recollection&amp;rft.pub=George+Banta+Publishing+Company&amp;rft.date=1938&amp;rft.aulast=Cameron&amp;rft.aufirst=Alister&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APhilosophy"></span></span>
