<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFGuthrie1962–1981">Guthrie, W. K. C. (1962–1981). <a class="external text" href="https://www.worldcat.org/oclc/22488892" rel="nofollow"><i>A history of Greek philosophy</i></a>. Cambridge: Cambridge University Press. pp. 165–166. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-05160-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-521-05160-6"><bdi>978-0-521-05160-6</bdi></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/22488892" rel="nofollow">22488892</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20220121185645/https://www.worldcat.org/title/history-of-greek-philosophy/oclc/22488892" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 21 January 2022<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">12 June</span> 2021</span>. <q>This does not of course amount to saying that the simile goes back to Pythagoras himself, but only that the Greek ideal of philosophia and theoria (for which we may compare Herodotus's attribution of these activities to Solon I, 30) was at a fairly early date annexed by the Pythagoreans for their master</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+history+of+Greek+philosophy&amp;rft.place=Cambridge&amp;rft.pages=165-166&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1962%2F1981&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F22488892&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-521-05160-6&amp;rft.aulast=Guthrie&amp;rft.aufirst=W.+K.+C.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.worldcat.org%2Foclc%2F22488892&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APhilosophy"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFAdler2000">Adler, Mortimer J. (2000). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Pv3BHyktJWkC" rel="nofollow"><i>How to Think About the Great Ideas: From the Great Books of Western Civilization</i></a>. Chicago, Ill.: Open Court. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8126-9412-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8126-9412-3"><bdi>978-0-8126-9412-3</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=How+to+Think+About+the+Great+Ideas%3A+From+the+Great+Books+of+Western+Civilization&amp;rft.place=Chicago%2C+Ill.&amp;rft.pub=Open+Court&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8126-9412-3&amp;rft.aulast=Adler&amp;rft.aufirst=Mortimer+J.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DPv3BHyktJWkC&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APhilosophy"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-justification-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-justification_14-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2" id="CITEREFQuinton"><a href="/wiki/Anthony_Quinton" title="Anthony Quinton">Quinton, Anthony</a>, <i>The ethics of philosophical practice</i>, p. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=sI4YAAAAIAAJ" rel="nofollow">666</a>, <q>Philosophy is rationally critical thinking, of a more or less <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/systematic" title="wikt:systematic">systematic</a> kind about the general nature of the world (metaphysics or theory of existence), the justification of belief (epistemology or theory of knowledge), and the conduct of life (ethics or theory of value). Each of the three elements in this list has a non-philosophical counterpart, from which it is distinguished by its explicitly rational and critical way of proceeding and by its systematic nature. Everyone has some general conception of the nature of the world in which they live and of their place in it. Metaphysics replaces the unargued assumptions embodied in such a conception with a rational and organized body of beliefs about the world as a whole. Everyone has occasion to doubt and question beliefs, their own or those of others, with more or less success and without any theory of what they are doing. Epistemology seeks by argument to make explicit the rules of correct belief formation. Everyone governs their conduct by directing it to desired or valued ends. Ethics, or moral philosophy, in its most inclusive sense, seeks to articulate, in rationally systematic form, the rules or principles involved.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+ethics+of+philosophical+practice&amp;rft.pages=666&amp;rft.aulast=Quinton&amp;rft.aufirst=Anthony&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APhilosophy"></span> in <a href="#CITEREFHonderich1995">Honderich 1995</a>.</span>
</li>
